Preference of Haqiqah vs Majaz in Oaths: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Explaining dispute on real vs metaphorical meanings in oath context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on haqiqah vs majaz in oaths, apply the principle of substitution (khalaf) to derive the practical legal outcome, and evaluate the underlying usul rationale.

Core Idea
In oaths, Abu Hanifa prefers literal meaning; Sahibayn prefer common metaphor — traced to substitution in speech vs ruling.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: when a word has both a real and a common metaphorical meaning, which governs an oath? Using examples such as swearing not to eat 'this wheat' or not to drink 'this Euphrates', Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) arrive at different conclusions. The dispute is traced to a fundamental principle: whether metaphor is a substitute for reality in speech (takallum) or in ruling (hukm). Understanding this helps clarify the interpretive method in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two meanings
Read the passage and note the example: an oath using a word with both a real meaning (haqiqah) and a common metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'arif). For instance, 'wheat' (al-hintah) literally means the grain, but metaphorically includes bread. Similarly, 'Euphrates' (al-furat) literally means drinking directly from the river, but metaphorically includes drinking from a vessel.
These examples are deliberately chosen to show the practical impact of the dispute.
2
State the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that acting upon the real meaning is preferable (al-hanafiyah). The Sahibayn hold that acting upon the general metaphorical meaning is preferable (al-umum al-majaz).
Memorize: Abu Hanifa → haqiqah; Sahibayn → majaz.
3
Trace the fundamental principle: khalaf in takallum vs khalaf in hukm
The dispute reduces to the nature of metaphor as a substitute (khalaf). Abu Hanifa sees metaphor as a substitute in speech (takallum): if the real expression is linguistically valid, it is used; metaphor is only employed when reality is impossible. The Sahibayn see metaphor as a substitute in ruling (hukm): metaphor encompasses the ruling of reality, thus it is more comprehensive and preferable.
This is a rational (aqli) argument, not based on a specific text.
4
Examine the example of the master and the older slave
Abu Hanifa's view is illustrated by a master calling an older slave 'my son' — a metaphor used correctly in speech even though the real relationship does not hold. This shows that in speech, the real meaning is not required; metaphor is valid as a linguistic tool, but preponderance is still given to the real meaning when possible.
This example clarifies that the preference for haqiqah is not absolute but based on preponderance.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the oath examples, Abu Hanifa restricts breach to literal consumption: eating the grain directly or drinking by mouth from the river. The Sahibayn include all customary forms: bread made from wheat or drinking from a vessel. The practical difference is clear in fatwa.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows how usul principles directly affect fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَ اللَّفْظُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ مُسْتَعْمَلَةٌ وَمَجَازٌ مُتَعَارَفْ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هذِهِ الْحِنْطَةِ أَوْ لَا يَشْرَبْ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْفُرَاتِ فَعِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ الْعَمَلُ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ أَوْلَى وَعِنْدَهُمَا الْعَمَلُ بِعُمُومِ الْمَجَازِ أَوْلَى. وَهَذَا يَرْجِعُ إِلَى أَصْلٍ وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْمَجَازَ خَلَفٌ عَنِ الْحَقِيقَةِ فِي التَّكَلُمِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ حَتَّى صَخَتِ الْإِسْتِعَارَةُ بِهِ عِنْدَهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَنْعَقِدْ لِإِيجَابِ الْحَقِيقَةِ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ لِعَبْدِهِ وَهُوَ أَكْبَرُ سِنًّا مِنْهُ هَذَا ابْنِي فَاعْتَبَرَ الرُّجْحَانَ فِي التَّكَلُمِ فَصَارَتِ الْحَقِيْقَةُ أَوْلى وَعِنْدَهُمَا الْمَجَازُ خَلَفٌ عَنِ الْحَقِيقَةِ فِي الْحُكْمِ وَفِي الْحُكْمِ لِلْمَجَازِ رُجْحَانٌ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى حُكْمِ الْحَقِيقَةِ فَصَارَ أَوْلى
If a word has a real meaning that is in use and a conventional metaphorical meaning, such as when one swears 'I will not eat from this wheat' or 'I will not drink from this Euphrates', then according to Abu Hanifa, acting upon the real meaning is preferable, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), acting upon the general metaphorical meaning is preferable. And this goes back to a principle: that metaphor is a substitute for reality in speech according to Abu Hanifa, such that using metaphor is valid in his view even if the real meaning is not intended, as in his saying to his slave who is older than him 'this is my son'. So he considers the preponderance in speech, thus the real meaning is preferable. According to the two, metaphor is a substitute for reality in ruling, and in ruling the metaphor is preponderant because it includes the ruling of the real meaning, thus it is preferable.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding the interpretation of a word that has both a real (haqiqah) and a common metaphorical (majaz muta'arif) meaning. The context is oaths: when one swears not to eat 'this wheat' (al-hintah), the real meaning is eating the grain itself (e.g., roasted or boiled), while the metaphorical meaning includes wheat-based products like bread. Similarly, swearing not to drink 'this Euphrates' (al-furat) has the real meaning of drinking directly from the river, and the metaphorical meaning of drinking from a vessel or cup. Abu Hanifa prefers the real meaning, so breaking the oath would only occur by eating the grain directly or drinking by mouth from the river. The Sahibayn prefer the common metaphor, so any form of consuming wheat (including bread) or drinking water from that source (even via a vessel) breaks the oath. The dispute is traced to a fundamental principle: whether metaphor is a substitute for reality in speech (takallum) or in ruling (hukm). Abu Hanifa holds the former: if the real expression is linguistically correct, it is preferred; if not possible in reality, one turns to metaphor to avoid nonsense. The Sahibayn hold the latter: since metaphor encompasses the ruling of reality, it is preferred. An example is given of a master saying 'this is my son' to an older slave; metaphor is used correctly in speech, but the real relationship is not intended. This structured analysis shows how usul principles directly affect fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
حَقِيقَة (ḥaqīqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as established in the language.
مَجَاز (majāz): The metaphorical, non-literal meaning of a word used in a customary context.
خَلَف (khalaf): A substitute for the original; here metaphor is a substitute for reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental principle that explains the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn?
A) Abu Hanifa considers metaphor valid only in poetry; Sahibayn consider it in all speech.
B) Abu Hanifa holds that metaphor is a substitute in speech; Sahibayn hold it is a substitute in ruling.
C) Both agree on the principle but disagree on the example.
D) The difference is based on Quranic verses, not rational inference.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, the preference of haqiqah over majaz (or vice versa) depends on whether metaphor is viewed as a substitute in speech or in ruling, directly affecting the scope of the oath.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Majaz: Caliph in Utterance or Ruling? The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on majaz as caliph of haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the locus of majaz (utterance vs. ruling) and apply their views to the example of a young master calling an old slave 'my son'.

Core Idea
Majaz substitutes for haqiqah: in utterance (Abu Hanifa) or in ruling (Sahibayn) — with a critical practical difference.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational usul dispute within the Hanafi school: the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage). Is majaz a deputy (khalifah) of the literal meaning in the act of speaking (takallum) or in the ruling (hukm) conveyed? This apparently theoretical question has a concrete legal consequence, as shown by the famous example of a twenty-year-old master who calls his sixty-year-old slave 'my son'. We will walk through the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), and understand how each view leads to a different ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What exactly is being disputed? Is the dispute about the definition of majaz or about its application conditions? The question is: in what sense is majaz a caliph (deputy) of haqiqah? In the utterance itself or in the ruling that the utterance conveys?
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that majaz is caliph in utterance (takallum). His proof: speech is primarily about the utterance; meaning follows. Therefore, if the literal meaning is impossible but the utterance is grammatically correct, we can shift to metaphorical meaning.
He uses the example: a young master saying 'my son' to an old slave is grammatically correct; thus it becomes majaz meaning 'free'.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn hold that majaz is caliph in ruling (hukm). Their proof: the substance of speech is meaning and ruling; the word is merely a means. Therefore, if the literal meaning is impossible in existence, the speech is futile (laghw) and cannot be turned to majaz.
For them, the example is futile; no legal effect.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Apply both views to the example: a 20-year-old master tells his 60-year-old slave 'this is my son'. Under Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave is freed because the statement is interpreted metaphorically. Under the Sahibayn, the statement is futile and the slave remains enslaved. This difference matters in actual fatwa.
5
Consider the tarjih (preponderance) discussion
The passage also discusses which interpretation is preferred when both literal and metaphorical meanings are possible. Imam Abu Hanifa prefers the literal if possible; the Sahibayn prefer the metaphorical because it is more comprehensive or more frequently used. This relates to the larger principle of interpreting ambiguous texts.
The commentator suggests that the author's phrase only covers general majaz, not customary majaz, and how one could expand it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور صاحبین کہتے ہیں کہ مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ حکم اور معنی میں ہے، کیونکہ کلام میں اصل معنی اور حکم ہی ہوتا ہے، لفظ تو صرف وسیلہ ہوتا ہے، لہذا جب حکم میں خلیفہ ہے تو حقیقت کا معنی کے لحاظ سے صحیح اور ممکن الوجود ہونا ضروری ہے، لہذا اگر حقیقی معنی ممکن الوجود ہو اور کسی عارض کی وجہ سے وہ معنی مراد نہ لئے جا سکے تو کلام کو مجاز کی طرف پھیرا جائے گا، اور اگر حقیقت کا معنی ممکن ہی نہ ہو تو کلام لغو ہو جائے گا اس کو مجاز کی طرف نہیں پھیرا جائے گا۔
مثال سے سمجھو ، ایک بیس سالہ مولی نے اپنے ساٹھ سالہ غلام سے کہا هذا ابنی یہ میرا بیٹا ہے، تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک یہ کلام عربیت اور ترکیب نحوی کے لحاظ سے صحیح ہے ، مبتدا خبر ہے ، اگر چہ اس کے معنی ممکن الوجود نہیں ہیں، کیونکہ باپ چھوٹا اور بیٹا عمر میں بڑا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے، مگر تکلم میں کلام صحیح ہے تو کلام کو اخو نہیں کریں گے بلکہ اس کو مجاز کی طرف پھیر دینگے اور اس کا مجازی معنی حریت ہے ، آزاد ہونا ہے، یہ مجاز اس لئے ہے کہ بنوت کو حریت لازم ہے، یعنی بیٹا ہمیشہ آزاد ہوتا ہے، پس هذا ابنی جب اس سے بیٹا ہونا مراد ہو ملزوم ہوا، اور ھذا ابنی جب اس سے آزاد ہونا مراد لیا جائے لازم ہوا ملزوم بولکر لازم مراد لیا اور یہی مجاز ہے، پس ” هذا ابنی“ سے هذاحر“ مراد لیا جائے گا اور وہ ساٹھ سالہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک "هذا ابنی“ کے معنی ہی صحیح نہیں ہیں، یہ ممکن الوجود ہی نہیں ، اس لئے کہ بیٹا عمر میں باپ سے بڑا نہیں ہوسکتا ہے، لہذا یہ کلام لغو ہو جائے گا ، اس کو مجاز کی طرف نہیں پھیرا جائے گا، کیونکہ مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ حکم میں ہے تو حقیقت کا حکم اور معنی ممکن ہونا ضروری ہے ، اور یہاں هذا ابنی کا معنی ممکن الوجود نہیں ہے، لہذا کلام لغو ہو جائے گا۔
واعتبر الرجحان - جب یہ اصل مختلف فیہ آپ سمجھ گئے کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ تکلم میں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک حکم میں خلیفہ ہے ، تو مصنف اب اصل مسئلہ میں اختلاف کا ذکر کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ امام صاحب تکلم میں ترجیح کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں یعنی اگر حقیقت پر عمل ممکن ہو تو تکلم بالحقیقہ تکلم بالمجاز کی بنسبت اولی اور راج ہے کیونکہ مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ تکلم میں ہے اور تکلم بالحقیقہ جب اس پر عمل ممکن بھی ہوتو یہ تکلم بالجاز پر راجج ہے، لہذا حقیقت مستعملہ کو مجاز متعارف پر ترجیح ہوگی۔
اور صاحبین کے نزدیک چونکہ مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ حکم کے لحاظ سے ہے، اور حکم میں مجاز کو ترجیح حاصل ہے،اس لئے کہ مجازی معنی ایسے عام ہیں کہ حقیقی معنی بھی اس میں داخل ہو جاتے ہیں ، جیسے عموم مجاز میں ، اور اگر مجاز متعارف ہے تو بھی مجاز کو ترجیح حاصل ہے اس لئے کہ مجاز حقیقت کی بنسبت اد مستعمل ہے ، لہذا مجاز مراد لینا زیادہ اولی ہو گا ، چاہے مجاز متعارف ہو یا عموم مجاز ہو۔
مصنف نے لاشتماله على حكم الحقیقة کہ کر عموم مجاز کی ترجیح کو بیان کر دیا ہجاز متعارف کی ترجیح بیان نہیں ہوئی اگر اس کے بعد اما لغلبة استعماله بڑھا دیتے تو مجاز متعارف کی ترجیح بھی واضح ہو جاتی ، یا صرف لغلبة استعمال کہتے تو بھی عموم مجاز اور مجاز متعارف دونوں کی ترجیح مفہوم ہو جاتی ، کیونکہ عموم مجاز اور مجاز متعارف دونوں کا استعمال حقیقت سے زیادہ ہے۔
And the Sahibayn say that majaz is the caliph of haqiqah in ruling and meaning, because in speech the original is meaning and ruling, and the word is merely a means. Therefore, when it is caliph in ruling, the meaning of haqiqah must be correct and possible in existence. So if the literal meaning is possible in existence but due to some impediment that meaning cannot be intended, then the speech will be turned towards majaz. And if the meaning of haqiqah is not possible at all, then the speech becomes futile and will not be turned towards majaz.
Understand by example: A twenty-year-old master said to his sixty-year-old slave: 'Hādhā ibnī' (This is my son). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this speech is correct in terms of Arabic and syntactic structure, it is mubtada' and khabar, although its meaning is not possible in existence because a father cannot be younger and a son older in age. However, in utterance the speech is correct, so we will not consider it futile but will turn it towards majaz. Its metaphorical meaning is freedom (hurriyyah). This majaz is because sonship necessarily implies freedom, i.e., a son is always free. So when 'hādhā ibnī' intends sonship, it is the malzūm; and when 'hādhā ibnī' intends freedom, the lāzim is intended by uttering the malzūm, and that is majaz. Thus, 'hādhā ibnī' will be taken to mean 'hādhā ḥurr' (this is free), and that sixty-year-old slave will become free. According to the Sahibayn, the meaning of 'hādhā ibnī' is not correct; it is not possible in existence because a son cannot be older than his father. Therefore, this speech will be futile and will not be turned towards majaz, because majaz is the caliph of haqiqah in ruling, so the ruling and meaning of haqiqah must be possible, and here the meaning of 'hādhā ibnī' is not possible in existence, so the speech is futile.
And consider the preponderance: Since you have understood this fundamental point of dispute that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, majaz is the caliph of haqiqah in utterance, and according to the Sahibayn, it is caliph in ruling, the author now mentions the difference in the main issue. He says that Imam Abu Hanifa considers preponderance in utterance, i.e., if acting upon the literal is possible, then uttering the literal is preferable and preponderant over uttering the metaphorical, because majaz is the caliph of haqiqah in utterance, and when uttering the literal is also possible, then uttering the literal is preponderant over uttering the metaphorical. Therefore, the literal in actual use will have preponderance over the customary metaphorical.
And according to the Sahibayn, since majaz is the caliph of haqiqah in terms of ruling, and in ruling the metaphorical has preponderance, because metaphorical meanings are so general that literal meanings also enter into them, like in general majaz. And if the majaz is customary, still the metaphorical has preponderance because the metaphorical is more frequently used than the literal. Therefore, intending the metaphorical is more preferable, whether it is customary majaz or general majaz.
The author, by saying 'li-ishtimālihi ʿalā ḥukmi l-ḥaqīqati' (because it includes the ruling of the literal), expressed the preponderance of general majaz. The preponderance of customary majaz was not expressed. If after it he had added 'aw li-ghalabati istiʿmālihi' (or due to the predominance of its usage), then the preponderance of customary majaz would also have been clarified. Or if he had said only 'li-ghalabati istiʿmālihi', then both general majaz and customary majaz would have been understood, because the usage of both general majaz and customary majaz is greater than that of the literal.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational usul dispute within the Hanafi school between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage). The issue is whether majaz is the caliph (deputy) of haqiqah (literal meaning) in utterance (takallum) or in ruling (hukm). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that majaz substitutes for haqiqah in the act of speaking (takallum), meaning that as long as the utterance is grammatically correct and the literal meaning is possible, the speech is valid and can be interpreted metaphorically. The Sahibayn hold that majaz substitutes in the ruling (hukm), meaning that the literal meaning itself must be possible for majaz to be invoked. The famous example illustrates the difference: a 20-year-old master says to his 60-year-old slave 'this is my son'. Literally impossible (son older than father). Imam Abu Hanifa says the statement is valid as majaz (meaning 'free'), so the slave is freed. The Sahibayn say the statement is futile (laghw) because the literal meaning is impossible, so no legal effect. The passage then discusses the principle of tarjih (preferring one interpretation over another). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the literal meaning can be acted upon, it is preponderant over the metaphorical. According to the Sahibayn, the metaphorical is preponderant because it includes more meanings or is more frequently used. The commentator notes that the author's phrase 'li-ishtimālihi ʿalā ḥukmi l-ḥaqīqati' only covers general majaz, and suggests adding 'aw li-ghalabati istiʿmālihi' to also cover customary majaz.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage of a word for a meaning other than its original literal meaning, with a relationship (like necessity) and a preventing factor.
حقیقت (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally placed for that meaning.
خلیفہ (khalīfah): Deputy or substitute; majaz substitutes for haqiqah in either utterance or ruling.
تکلم (takallum): The act of speaking; the utterance itself as the locus of substitution according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
حکم (ḥukm): The legal ruling or effect; the meaning of the speech as the locus of substitution according to Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a young master calling his old slave 'my son', what is the consequence according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The slave does not become free because the statement is futile.
B) The slave becomes free because the statement is taken as majaz meaning 'free'.
C) The statement is invalid because it is a lie.
D) The master must clarify his intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal dispute on majaz shows that the same legal utterance can be interpreted differently based on whether majaz is seen as substituting in utterance or ruling, affecting actual rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Prioritizing Isharah over Dalalah in Conflicting Texts
Usul dispute on precedence of isharah over dalalah with Quranic example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that isharah is given precedence over dalalah when they conflict, using the intentional killing kaffarah dispute as a case study.

Core Idea
When a text's isharah and dalalah conflict, isharah wins because it is a stronger indication of intent.

In Hanafi usul, both isharah and dalalah are definitive (qat'i) indications from the text, but when they point to contradictory rulings, the jurists must decide which prevails. This lesson examines a classic dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over whether intentional killing requires expiation. The Hanafi position relies on the isharah of Quran 4:93, while the Shafi'i position uses the dalalah of Quran 4:92. Understanding the hierarchy of textual indications is key to resolving such conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting proofs
Locate the two Quranic verses at the heart of the dispute: one on accidental killing (4:92) and one on intentional killing (4:93). Determine the type of indication each provides.
Verse 4:92 mentions kaffarah for accidental killing; verse 4:93 mentions Hell as recompense for intentional killing.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i dalalah
Understand how the Shafi'i school derives kaffarah for intentional killing from verse 4:92 via a fortiori (qiyas awla). They argue that if accidental killing requires expiation, then intentional killing, being a greater sin, must also require it.
This is an inference from the text, not the explicit wording—hence it is dalalah.
3
Analyze the Hanafi isharah
Examine verse 4:93: the word 'jaza'' implies sufficiency. Hanafis argue that since Hell is described as the complete recompense, no additional kaffarah is needed. This is an isharah from the text.
Isharah is based on the underlying meaning of the word choice, not explicit wording.
4
Apply the principle of tarjih
Since isharah and dalalah conflict, the Hanafi rule is that isharah takes precedence. Thus, intentional killing does not require kaffarah.
Both indications are qat'i, but isharah is considered stronger in revealing the text's intended meaning.
5
Address the objection
Consider the apparent contradiction: if Hell is sufficient, why do Hanafis still prescribe qisas/diya? The answer distinguishes between the act's recompense (Hell) and the subject's right (worldly penalties). Qisas is established by another text, not from this verse.
This shows that the isharah does not negate other established rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الا عند التعارض يہاں یہ بتا رہے ہیں کہ دلالت النص اور اشارة النص دونوں قطعی ہیں مگر تعارض کے وقت اشارۃ النص کو تر جیح ہوگی۔ تعارض کی مثال :- اللہ تعالیٰ کا ارشاد ہے، مَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَأً فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتل خطا میں کفارہ ہے، یہ تو عبارۃ النص سے ثابت ہوا اور اس آیت میں دلالتہ النص سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتل عمد میں بھی کفارہ ہے، کیونکہ قتل عمد قتل خطا سے بڑا جرم ہے، تو جب ادنی میں ہے تو اعلی میں بدرجہ اولی کفارہ ہوگا جیسا کہ امام شافعی لینے کا یہی مذہب ہے اور وہ اس سے استدلال کرتے ہیں ، بہر حال اس آیت کے دلالت سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتل عمد میں کفارہ ہے اور دوسری آیت میں اللہ تعالیٰ ارشاد ہے وَمَنْ يَقْتُلُ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمَدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمَ خَالِدًا فِيهَا۔ اس آیت کے اشارۃ النص سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتل عمد میں کفارہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ اللہ تعالی نے جزاء کا لفظ استعمال کیا ہے، اور جزا کے معنی کافی یعنی قتل عمد کی جزا جو بیان کی کہ جہنم ہے وہ کافی جزاء ہے، اس کے علاوہ اور کچھ نہیں ہے جیسا کہ ہمارا مذہب ہے۔ تو تعارض ہو گیا دلالت النص اور اشارۃ النص میں لہذا اشارۃ النص کو ترجیح ہوگی، پس قتل عمد میں کفارہ نہیں ہو گا۔ احناف کے مسلک پر ایک اعتراض ہو سکتا ہے کہ جب پوری جزا جہنم ہے ، تو پھر قتل عمد میں قصاص اور بعض صورتوں میں دیت ( جب باپ بیٹے کو قتل کرے یا قاتل اولیاء مقتول سے صلح کر لے ) کیوں واجب کرتے ہیں۔ جواب یہ دیا جائے گا کہ یہاں دو چیز میں الگ الگ ہیں، ایک فعل اور ایک محل، آیت میں جہنم جو جز ابتائی گئی ہے وہ فعل کی جزاء ہے اور وہ پوری جزاء ہے، اور قصاص اور دیت محل کی یعنی مقتول کی جزاء ہے جو اولیا کا حق ہے۔ دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ قصاص دوسری نص سے ثابت ہے و كتبنا عليهم فيها ان النفس بالنفس والعين بالعین آیت سے۔
Except when there is conflict: Here it is explained that both dalalah and isharah of the text are definitive, but at the time of conflict, isharah of the text is given precedence. An example of conflict: Allah's saying: 'Whoever kills a believer by mistake, then freeing a slave.' From this verse, it is understood that accidental killing requires expiation—this is established by 'ibarah of the text. And from the dalalah of this verse, it is understood that intentional killing also requires expiation, because intentional killing is a greater crime; if it applies to the lesser, then to the greater it is more deserving (a fortiori). This is the school of Imam al-Shafi'i, and they use this as evidence. Anyway, from the dalalah of this verse it is understood that intentional killing has expiation. And in another verse, Allah says: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein.' From the isharah of this verse, it is understood that there is no expiation for intentional killing because Allah used the word 'jaza'' (recompense), and jaza' means sufficient: the recompense He mentioned, i.e., Hell, is sufficient; there is nothing beyond it, as is our school. So there is a conflict between the dalalah and the isharah; therefore, isharah is given precedence, and thus intentional killing has no expiation. An objection may be raised against the Hanafi position: if the full recompense is Hell, then why do they make qisas (retaliation) obligatory in intentional killing, and in some cases diya (blood money) when a father kills his son or when the killer compromises with the heirs? The first answer: these are two separate matters: one is the act, the other is the subject. The Hell mentioned in the verse is the recompense for the act, and that is the full recompense. Qisas and diya are recompense for the subject, i.e., the slain person, which is the right of the heirs. The second answer: qisas is established by another text: 'And We prescribed for them therein: a life for a life, an eye for an eye,' etc.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on the methodology of prioritizing textual indications when they conflict. The example is intentional killing: Shafi'is use the dalalah of the verse on accidental killing (a fortiori) to deduce expiation for intentional killing. Hanafis counter with the isharah of the verse on intentional killing: the word 'jaza'' implies sufficiency, so no expiation beyond Hell. Since isharah is considered a stronger form of textual indication than dalalah (though both are qat'i), it is given precedence. The passage then addresses an apparent contradiction: if intentional killing only merits Hell, why does Hanafi law prescribe qisas or diya? The answer distinguishes between the recompense for the act (ukhrawi, for the Hereafter) and the recompense for the subject (dunyawi, for the heirs). Qisas and diya are worldly penalties owed to the victim's heirs, not additional punishment for the act itself. Thus, there is no conflict.

Key Terms
إشارة النص (ishārat al-nass): The indication from the text that points to a ruling through underlying meaning (like sufficiency implied by 'jaza'').
تعارض (ta‘āruḍ): Conflict between two proofs that cannot both be applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis give precedence to isharah over dalalah when they conflict?
A) Because isharah is more explicit in wording.
B) Because isharah is considered a stronger indication of the text's intent.
C) Because dalalah is not a valid proof at all.
D) Because the majority of scholars do so.

Key Takeaway
When a text's isharah and dalalah conflict, the Hanafi school gives precedence to isharah, as demonstrated by the intentional killing kaffarah dispute.

Second Invalid Method: Conditions and Attributes Imply Negation
Introduces Shafi'i view and example, sets up Hanafi critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i claim that conditions negate rulings in their absence, identify the Quranic example, and apply the Hanafi critique that conditions may have other purposes.

Core Idea
A condition does not always negate the ruling in its absence — it may clarify the ruling's applicability for other reasons.

In this passage, we encounter one of the main points of contention between Hanafi and Shafi'i usul: whether a conditional expression (shart) or a specific attribute (wasf) necessarily negates the ruling when absent. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it does, citing the Quranic verse on marriage to slave women. The Hanafi school considers this method invalid, as we will see. This lesson dissects the Shafi'i position and prepares the ground for the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i claim
When a ruling is suspended on a condition or attributed to a specific description, the absence of that condition or description necessitates negation of the ruling.
This is the principle being scrutinized.
2
Identify the Shafi'i proof
The Quranic verse about marrying slave women (Quran 4:25) is cited as evidence.
The condition is inability to afford free believing women.
3
Analyze the inference
Shafi'i reads the condition as a necessary prerequisite for the permission; therefore, when the condition is absent, the permission is negated.
The linguistic link: conditional 'fa' indicates result following condition.
4
Contrast the Hanafi view
The Hanafi school holds that this method is invalid. Conditions may be for clarification, emphasis, or to indicate a cause (sabab), not necessarily to negate.
The full Hanafi response is not given in this passage but is part of the broader discussion.
5
Assess the practical consequence
This dispute affects whether marriage to a slave woman is permissible when one can afford a free woman. Shafi'i says no; Hanafis may allow it under certain circumstances.
This is an example of thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا مَا قَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ لا اِنَ الْحُكْمَ مَتى عَلِقَ بِشَرْطِ أَوْ أَضِيْفَ إِلى مُسَمًّى ته بِوَصْفٍ خَاصَ أَوْجَبَ نَفْى الحُكْم عِنْدَ عَدَم الشَّرْطِ اَوِ الْوَصْفِ وَلِهَذَا لَمْ يُجَوِزْنِ نِكَاحَ الْآمَةِ عِنْدَ فَوَاتِ الشَّرْطِ اَوِ الْوَصْفِ الْمَذْكُورَيْنِ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُم طَوْلاً أَنْ يَنْكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَتِ الْمُؤمِنَاتِ فَمِمَّا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ مِنْ فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ الله. عالحما ترجمہ: اور ان وجوہ فاسدہ میں سے ایک وہ ہے جو امام شافعی اہلیہ نے فرمایا کہ حکم جب کسی شرط کے ساتھ معلق ہو، یا حکم کسی مسمی کی طرف منسوب ہو کسی وصف خاص کے ساتھ تو یہ شرط یا وصف کے نہ ہونے کے وقت حکم کی نفی
And among them is what al-Shafi'i said: 'Indeed, when a ruling is suspended on a condition or attributed to a named thing with a specific attribute, it necessitates negation of the ruling when the condition or attribute is absent.' And for this reason, he does not permit marriage to a slave woman when the condition or attribute mentioned in the verse of Allah تعالى is absent: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then [let him marry] from what your right hands possess of believing slave girls.' Translation: And among these invalid reasons is what Imam al-Shafi'i said: 'When a ruling is suspended on a condition, or a ruling is attributed to a named thing with a specific attribute, then at the time of the absence of the condition or attribute, it necessitates negation of the ruling.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage introduces the second invalid method according to the Hanafi school. Imam al-Shafi'i is reported to have argued that if a ruling is conditioned upon a specific condition or attributed to a specific description, then the absence of that condition or description implies the negation of the ruling. As an example, Shafi'i cites the Quranic verse about marrying slave women: the verse makes permission conditional upon not being able to afford free believing women. He interprets this to mean that when the condition (inability to afford) is absent, the permission to marry slave women is negated. The Hanafi position, which would be elaborated in the following text (but is cut off here), is that this method is invalid. The text ends abruptly, and the full Hanafi refutation is not provided in this window. The commentary would typically argue that the condition or description is only mentioned to clarify the ruling's applicability when present, but it does not necessarily negate the ruling when absent; other factors may apply.

Key Terms
تعليق الحكم بالشرط (ta'liq al-hukm bi-al-shart): Suspending a ruling upon a condition.
الوصف الخاص (al-wasf al-khas): A specific attribute or description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i principle that conditions always negate rulings in their absence is contested; the Hanafi school holds that conditions may be explanatory rather than exclusive.

Ta'liq bil-Shart: Does the Condition Block the Cause or the Ruling?
Explaining the dispute on ta'liq bil-shart and its first subsidiary issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on ta'liq bil-shart and apply the difference to the scenario of conditional divorce on future marriage.

Core Idea
Ta'liq bil-shart prevents the cause for Hanafis but only the ruling for Shafi'is — leading to different rulings on its validity.

When a legal ruling is suspended on a future condition, does the condition block the existence of the cause itself, or only delay the effect? This usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools has direct fiqh consequences. In this lesson, we examine the core disagreement and its first subsidiary issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core disagreement
State the issue: does ta'liq bil-shart prevent the cause (sabab) or the ruling (hukm)? Note the Hanafi and Shafi'i views.
2
Analyze Shafi'i's proof via analogy
Examine the lamp analogy: heaviness (sabab) remains, rope (condition) prevents falling (hukm). Understand how this supports Shafi'i's view that cause is concluded.
The analogy is physical, not legal, as the Hanafis point out.
3
Study the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that in legal causes, if the cause cannot produce its effect immediately, it is not considered a cause. Reject the analogy as irrelevant.
4
Apply to the subsidiary issue
Consider the case: 'If I marry you, you are divorced' to a non-mahram woman. Apply each school's view to see if the statement is valid or void.
This is the first subordinate issue mentioned in the text.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude the practical difference: Hanafi allows conditional divorce on future marriage; Shafi'i declares it void.

فی الحال حکم ثابت نہ ہوگا بلکہ شرط کے زمانے تک مؤخر ہوگا لیکن مانع سبب نہیں ہے، لہذا سبب تو فوراً منعقد ہو جائے گا، اور یہاں چونکہ غیر کا غلام ہے جو آزادی کا محل نہیں، اور اجنبیہ عورت ہے جو سبب یعنی طلاق کا محل نہیں ہے تو سبب یعنی ( انت طالق اور انت حر لغو ہو جائے گا۔ اور ہمارے نزدیک چونکہ تعلیق بالشرط مانع سبب ہے یعنی سبب کے انعقاد کو روکتی ہے، پھر جب شرط پائی جائے گی تو اس وقت یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا اور حکم کا ثبوت ہوگا ، لہذا جب یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا اس وقت محل کی ضرورت پڑے گی ، ابھی محل کی ضرورت نہیں ہے، لہذا کلام لغو نہیں ہوگا، بلکہ جب شرط پائی جائے گی اس وقت یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا، اور جب یہ سبب منعقد ہو گا اس وقت محل موجود ہوگا، لہذا جب نکاح کرے گا یا غلام کا مالک بنے گا، تو اس وقت چونکہ محل موجود ہے لہذا اس وقت طلاق اور آزادی واقع ہو جائیگی۔
And [the discussion] of the obligation of its performance. As for the bodily [expiation], it does not admit separation. So when the performance was delayed, the obligation did not remain. Translation: And its conclusion is that Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, attached the description to the condition and considered ta'liq bil-shart as preventing the ruling, not the cause. And for this reason, Imam al-Shafi'i declared divorce and manumission suspended on ownership as invalid, and permitted giving expiation through wealth before breaking the oath, because according to his principle, obligation is established from the cause, and the obligation of performance is delayed from the caused due to the condition. And monetary expiation admits separation between the obligation itself and the obligation of performance. However, bodily expiation does not admit separation. So when performance was delayed, the obligation did not remain. Elaboration: The author says that the summary of Imam al-Shafi'i's statement are two things: (1) He attached the description to the condition, meaning just as the ruling is not established without the condition, similarly the ruling is not established without the description. (2) Ta'liq bil-shart is a preventer unanimously, but it prevents the ruling or the cause? Imam al-Shafi'i says it prevents the ruling, i.e., it prevents the ruling from being established until the condition occurs, but the cause is still concluded. For example, the condition of option in a sale prevents the ruling not the cause. Likewise, the analogy of a heavy lamp tied with a rope: the heaviness is the cause of falling, falling is the ruling, and the rope is like the condition. The rope does not remove the cause (heaviness) but prevents the ruling (falling). According to Imam al-Shafi'i, in case of ta'liq, the cause exists and is effective in establishing the ruling, but the ruling is delayed until the condition. According to us (Hanafis), ta'liq prevents the cause, i.e., the cause itself is not concluded. Therefore, when the cause is absent, the ruling is also absent. Hence, the author mentions two subsidiary issues: (1) If someone says to a non-mahram woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced' or to a slave not owned 'if I own you, you are free', in both cases according to Imam al-Shafi'i the statement is void; neither will divorce occur after marriage nor manumission after ownership. Because according to him, ta'liq prevents the ruling, not the cause, so the cause is concluded immediately, but here the cause (divorce/manumission) has no valid locus (mahall) at present, so it becomes futile. According to us (Hanafis), since ta'liq prevents the cause, when the condition occurs, then the cause will be concluded and the ruling will be established. At that time, the locus will be present, so the statement is not futile; divorce or manumission will occur when the condition is met.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the nature of ta'liq bil-shart (suspension by a condition). The author first summarizes Imam al-Shafi'i's position in two points: (1) a description (wasf) is treated like a condition in preventing the ruling, and (2) ta'liq prevents the ruling (hukm) but not the cause (sabab). Shafi'i supports this with the analogy of a heavy lamp: the heaviness (sabab) remains, but the rope (condition) prevents the falling (hukm). The Hanafi position opposes this: ta'liq prevents the cause itself, so the cause is not concluded until the condition is met. The commentator then elaborates the first subsidiary issue: if a man says to a non-mahram woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced', or to a slave not owned 'if I own you, you are free', the Shafi'i view declares the statement void because the cause (divorce/manumission) is immediate but lacks a valid locus (mahall). The Hanafi view declares it valid because the cause is postponed until the condition, at which point the locus exists. This showcases how the usul difference leads to different fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
تعلیق بالشرط (ta'liq bil-shart): Suspending a legal ruling (e.g., divorce, manumission) on the occurrence of a future condition.
سبب (sabab): The cause that leads to a legal ruling; in this context, the statement of divorce or manumission itself.
حکم (hukm): The legal effect or ruling, such as the actual occurrence of divorce or manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, if a man says to a non-mahram woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' what is the ruling?
A) The statement is void and divorce never occurs.
B) The statement is valid and divorce occurs immediately upon the statement.
C) The statement is valid and divorce occurs only after marriage.
D) The statement is valid but requires a new declaration after marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on ta'liq bil-shart — that the condition prevents the cause — allows conditional statements on future events to be valid, unlike the Shafi'i view.

Shafi'i Usul: Paying Kaffarah Before Hanth
Explaining the Shafi'i view on paying expiation before hanth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Shafi'i rationale for allowing expiation before breaking an oath and distinguish it from the Hanafi position by identifying the differing sabab (cause) of obligation.

Core Idea
Shafi'i: the oath itself causes obligation; hanth is only a condition for performance.

This passage extends our analysis of ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh. We saw earlier that Hanafis and Shafi'is differ on whether a suspended condition (ta'liq) can be the cause of a ruling. Here we see a practical branch: can one pay kaffarah for an oath before breaking it? The Shafi'i answer, based on their distinctive theory of causes and conditions, is yes—but only for monetary expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize the precise legal question: Is it valid to pay kaffarah for an oath before the oath is broken (hanth)?
This is a subsidiary issue (fur'u) derived from the usul principle of sabab vs shart.
2
State the Shafi'i Ruling and Its Basis
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, paying monetary expiation before hanth is valid. The basis is that the oath itself is the cause (sabab) of obligation, while hanth is only a condition (shart) for performance.
The passage explicitly contrasts this with the Hanafi view (implied) where the broken oath is the cause.
3
Compare with the Hanafi Position
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of kaffarah does not arise until the oath is actually broken. Thus paying before hanth would be invalid. The difference hinges on what constitutes the sabab.
The Hanafi position is not detailed in this passage but is understood from earlier discussion on ta'liq.
4
Apply the Principle to Monetary vs Bodily Expiation
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts this ruling to monetary expiation (freeing a slave/feeding the poor). Bodily expiation (fasting) cannot be performed before hanth because bodily acts do not admit separation between obligation and performance.
This distinction is crucial: the capacity to separate obligation from performance is unique to monetary duties.
5
Evaluate the Analogy with Zakat
The Shafi'i argument analogizes to zakat: ownership of nisab is the cause, haul is the condition for payment. Similarly, the oath is the cause, hanth is the condition. This analogy supports the validity of early payment.
The commentator uses this analogy to illustrate the usul principle.

وجوزالتکفیر سےدوسرا تفریعی مسئلہ ذکر کرتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی واللہ لا افعل كذاء اللہ کی قسم میں ایسا نہیں کرونگا اور حانث ہونے سے پہلے ہی کفارہ مالیہ ادا کر دیا یعنی ایک غلام آزاد کیا یا دس مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلا دیا تو جائز ہے، حانث ہونے کے بعد دوبارہ کفارہ نہیں دینا پڑے گا، کیونکہ امام شافعی کے نزدیک کفارے کے نفس وجوہ کا سبب یمین ہے، یعنی یمین ہوتے ہی کفارہ کا نفس وجوب ثابت ہو جاتا ہے،اسی لئے کفارہ کو یمین کی طرف مضاف کر کے کفارہ یمین کہا جاتا ہے اور حانث ہونا کفارے کے وجوب ادا کے لئے شرط ہے، لہذا امام شافعی کے اصول کے مطابق بعد انعقا دسبب قبل وجود الشرط حکم کی ادائیگی صحیح ہے ، جیسے نصاب کا مالک ہوتے ہی حولان حول سے پہلے زکوۃ ادا کر دی تو صحیح ہے، بعد میں اعادہ واجب نہیں ہے، تو اسی طرح یمین نفس وجوب کا سبب ہے، اور حانث ہونا کفارہ کے وجوب ادا کے لئے شرط ہے، لہذا سبب یعنی یمین پائے جانے کے بعد شرط یعنی حانث ہونے سے پہلے اگر کوئی کفارہ دے دے تو صحیح ہے، مگر امام شافعی پہلے کے نزدیک بھی شرط ہے کہ یہ صرف مالی کفارے میں ہے یعنی غلام آزاد کرنا یا دس مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلانا، ورنہ روزہ سے کفارہ حانث ہونے سے پہلے ان کے نزدیک بھی جائز نہیں ہے۔ والمالى يحتمل سے مصنف نے مالی اور بدنی کفارہ میں فرق بتا رہے ہیں ، یعنی امام شافعی کے بتارہے نزد یک مالی کفاره قبل الحنث جائز ہے اور بدنی کفارہ جائز نہیں ، کیوں؟ تو کہتے ہیں کہ واجب مالی میں نفس وجوب اور وجوب ادا میں فصل ہو سکتا ہے جیسے کسی نے ایک مہینہ کے وعدہ پر کوئی چیز خریدی تو شمن کا نفس وجوب تو عقد ہوتے ہی
And 'he allowed expiation' refers to the second subsidiary issue: if someone swears an oath saying 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such' and before breaking the oath he pays monetary expiation, i.e., frees a slave or feeds ten poor people, then it is valid, and he does not have to repeat the expiation after breaking the oath. Because according to Imam al-Shafi'i, the cause of the very obligation of expiation is the oath; i.e., as soon as the oath is taken, the obligation of expiation is established. For this reason, expiation is attributed to the oath and called 'kaffarah al-yamin'. Breaking the oath is a condition for the obligation of performance. Therefore, according to Imam al-Shafi'i's principle, after the cause is concluded but before the condition occurs, performing the ruling is valid, like paying zakat before the passing of a year after owning the nisab. Similarly, the oath is the cause of the obligation, and breaking the oath is a condition for the obligation of performance; so if one gives expiation after the cause (oath) but before the condition (hanth), it is valid. But according to Imam al-Shafi'i, this is only in monetary expiation (freeing a slave or feeding ten poor); expiation by fasting before hanth is not valid even according to him. And 'al-mali yah tamal' - the author explains the difference between monetary and bodily expiation: according to Imam al-Shafi'i, monetary expiation before hanth is valid, bodily is not. Why? Because in monetary obligations, there can be separation between the obligation itself and the obligation of performance, like someone who buys something with a promise of payment after a month; the obligation of price is established immediately upon contract, but performance is delayed.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second subsidiary issue arising from the Shafi'i position on ta'liq bil-shart. It addresses the validity of paying kaffarah (expiation) for an oath before actually breaking the oath (hanth). According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the oath itself is the cause (sabab) for the obligation of expiation; breaking the oath is only a condition (shart) for the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada). Therefore, paying the expiation before breaking the oath is valid, similar to paying zakat before the completion of one year of ownership. However, this applies only to monetary expiation (freeing a slave or feeding ten poor people), not to bodily expiation like fasting, because bodily acts do not admit separation between the obligation and its performance. The commentator then explains the distinction using the example of credit purchase: the obligation of price is immediate, but its performance can be deferred. This reinforces the Shafi'i usul principle that the cause establishes the obligation immediately, while the condition only governs the time of performance.

Key Terms
کفارہ یمین (kaffarah al-yamin): The expiation required for breaking a solemn oath (yamin).
حنث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath by doing what was sworn not to do.
مالی (mali): Monetary or property-based expiation, such as freeing a slave or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only bodily expiation (fasting).
B) Only monetary expiation (freeing slave/feeding poor).
C) Both monetary and bodily expiation.
D) Neither monetary nor bodily expiation.

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i school allows paying monetary expiation before breaking an oath because they consider the oath the cause of obligation, while the Hanafi school does not.

Ta'liq bi al-Shart: Does the Condition Block the Cause or the Ruling?
Inter-madhhab usul dispute on ta'liq bi al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on ta'liq bi al-shart, apply the concept of mani' sabab vs mani' hukm, and derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for conditional divorce and emancipation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a condition prevents the cause (sabab) itself, not just the ruling, preserving the validity of the suspension.

This passage dives into a foundational usul dispute: does a suspended condition block the legal cause (sabab) or only the effect (hukm)? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools answer differently, leading to opposite rulings in conditional divorce and manumission. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing sound legal analogies and issuing correct fatwas in cases of suspended legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools' interpretations of ta'liq bi al-shart
Read the passage and note how each school defines the role of the condition: Shafi'i sees it as a barrier to the ruling (mani' hukm), while Hanafi sees it as a barrier to the cause (mani' sabab).
This difference is not semantic; it determines whether the cause contracts immediately or is deferred.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i argument and its consequence
Follow the Shafi'i reasoning: if the cause contracts immediately, it must have a locus (mahall) at that moment. In the given examples, the mahall is absent (the woman is not a wife, the slave is not owned), so the cause becomes futile and the utterance is void.
Shafi'i thus holds that no divorce or emancipation ever occurs.
3
Examine the Hanafi response and the principle of mani' sabab
According to the Hanafi view, the condition prevents the cause itself from contracting. Therefore, the cause remains dormant until the condition is fulfilled. At that point, the mahall is present (the woman becomes a wife, the slave becomes owned), and the cause contracts validly, producing its effect.
This avoids the futility (laghw) that would arise if the cause contracted without a mahall.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Compare the outcomes: In the Hanafi school, the conditional utterance is valid and the effect occurs upon fulfillment of the condition. In the Shafi'i school, the utterance is void and no effect ever arises, even if the condition is later met.
This difference has significant implications for drafting marriage contracts, wills, and conditional gifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہے کہ کسی اجنبی عورت سے کہا ان نکحتک فانت طالق ، یا اجنبی کے غلام سے کہا ان ملکتک فانت حر ،تو دونوں صورتوں میں امام شافعی کے نزدیک کلام باطل ہو جائے گا یعنی نہ نکاح کے بعد عورت پر طلاق پڑے گی ، نہ مالک ہونے کے بعد غلام آزاد ہوگا، کیونکہ امام شافعی کے نزدیک تعلیق بالشرط مانع حکم ہے، یعنی فی الحال حکم ثابت نہ ہوگا بلکہ شرط کے زمانے تک مؤخر ہوگا لیکن مانع سبب نہیں ہے، لہذا سبب تو فوراً منعقد ہو جائے گا، اور یہاں چونکہ غیر کا غلام ہے جو آزادی کا محل نہیں، اور اجنبیہ عورت ہے جو سبب یعنی طلاق کا محل نہیں ہے تو سبب یعنی ( انت طالق اور انت ) حر لغو ہو جائے گا۔
اور ہمارے نزدیک چونکہ تعلیق بالشرط مانع سبب ہے یعنی سبب کے انعقاد کو روکتی ہے، پھر جب شرط پائی جائے گی تو اس وقت یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا اور حکم کا ثبوت ہوگا ، لہذا جب یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا اس وقت محل کی ضرورت پڑے گی ، ابھی محل کی ضرورت نہیں ہے، لہذا کلام لغو نہیں ہوگا، بلکہ جب شرط پائی جائے گی اس وقت یہ سبب منعقد ہوگا، اور جب یہ سبب منعقد ہو گا اس وقت محل موجود ہوگا، لہذا جب نکاح کرے گا یا غلام کا مالک بنے گا، تو اس وقت چونکہ محل موجود ہے لہذا اس وقت طلاق اور آزادی واقع ہو جائیگی۔
It is (said): if a man says to an unrelated woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced', or to a slave of someone else 'if I own you, you are free', then in both cases according to Imam Shafi'i the speech becomes void, meaning after marriage the divorce does not occur, nor after ownership does the slave become free, because according to Imam Shafi'i ta'liq bi al-shart is a barrier to the ruling (mani' hukm), i.e., the ruling is not established immediately but deferred until the time of the condition, but it is not a barrier to the cause (mani' sabab), so the cause contracts immediately, and here since the slave is owned by another and is not a locus for emancipation, and the woman is unrelated and is not a locus for the cause of divorce, therefore the cause (i.e., 'you are divorced' and 'you are free') becomes futile. And according to us, since ta'liq bi al-shart is a barrier to the cause (mani' sabab), i.e., it prevents the contraction of the cause, then when the condition occurs, the cause will contract at that time and the ruling will be established. So when the cause contracts, the need for a locus (mahall) arises; there is no need for a locus now. Therefore the speech is not futile; rather, when the condition occurs, the cause contracts, and when the cause contracts, the locus exists. So when he marries or becomes owner of the slave, since the locus exists, divorce and emancipation occur at that time.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab usul dispute regarding the function of ta'liq bi al-shart (suspension by condition). The Shafi'i position is that a condition prevents the ruling (hukm) but not the cause (sabab), so the cause contracts immediately. In the given examples, since the locus (mahall) for the effect is absent at the time of the utterance (the woman is not a wife, the slave is not owned), the cause becomes futile and the suspension is void. The Hanafi position, as stated by the author, is that the condition prevents the cause itself from contracting. Therefore, the cause remains dormant until the condition is fulfilled. At that moment, the cause contracts and the locus is present, so the effect (divorce, emancipation) occurs validly. This difference stems from a fundamental usul principle: whether the condition acts on the ruling or on the cause. The Hanafi view is preferred in the text.

Key Terms
تعلیق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a legal effect upon the occurrence of a future condition.
مانع سبب (mani' sabab): A barrier that prevents the cause from contracting.
محل (mahall): The locus upon which the legal effect operates (e.g., wife for divorce, slave for emancipation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, if a man says to an unrelated woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced', what happens when he later marries her?
A) The utterance is void; no divorce occurs.
B) The condition prevents the cause; upon marriage, the cause contracts and divorce occurs.
C) The divorce occurs immediately at the time of utterance.
D) It depends on the woman's consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, ta'liq bi al-shart acts as a barrier to the cause (mani' sabab), not merely the ruling, ensuring that the legal effect arises only when its locus is present, thereby avoiding futility.

Ta'liq bil-Shart: Preventing the Cause, Not the Ruling – A Hanafi Response to the Shafi'i Analogy
Hanafi usul dispute: is ta'liq mani' sabab or mani' hukm?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that ta'liq bil-shart prevents the cause (sabab) in divorce/manumission and distinguish this from khiyar al-shart in sale, using the concept of qiyas ma' al-fariq.

Core Idea
In divorce, suspension blocks the cause; in sale, it blocks the ruling — the legal nature differs fundamentally.

This passage tackles a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a conditional suspension (ta'liq bil-shart) prevent the cause (sabab) from arising, or does it merely prevent the ruling (hukm) while the cause remains? The Hanafi position, defended here, is that in acts of removal (isqat) like divorce and manumission, the condition blocks the cause itself. The Shafi'i view analogizes to khiyar al-shart in sale, where the condition blocks only the ruling. By working through an agreed case (an oath not to divorce followed by a suspended divorce), the author demonstrates the inconsistency of the Shafi'i view and establishes the Hanafi position through a critical distinction between affirmation and removal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and identify the point of dispute
Read the first part of the passage where the author explains that the condition enters upon the cause, not the ruling. Note the key terms: mani' sabab vs. mani' hukm.
The Hanafi position is that ta'liq prevents the cause; the Shafi'i holds it prevents the ruling.
2
Analyze the agreed case (oath + suspended divorce)
Examine the scenario: a man swears not to divorce, then says 'if you enter the house, you are divorced'. According to the Shafi'i view, the cause exists, so divorce should occur and break the oath. But both schools agree divorce does not occur. Work through the Hanafi reasoning that this proves the cause was prevented.
This case is 'mujma' alayh' (agreed upon) in terms of the outcome.
3
Understand the Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i analogy
The Shafi'i analogizes divorce to khiyar al-shart in sale. The author responds with 'qiyas ma' al-fariq' (false analogy). Identify the two distinctions: (a) sale is ithbat (affirmation), where suspension implies gambling; (b) divorce is isqat (removal), where no gambling is implied.
The hadith forbidding conditional sales is cited to show that khiyar al-shart is an exception by necessity.
4
Distinguish the operation of khiyar al-shart in sale
In khiyar al-shart, the condition enters upon the ruling (ownership), not the cause (contract). The author explains why this is necessary due to the prohibition of gambling and the need to prevent fraud.
If someone swears not to sell and then sells with khiyar, he breaks his oath because the sale is still contracted.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi usul rule
Combine the findings: for acts of removal (isqat), ta'liq prevents the cause; for acts of affirmation (ithbat), it prevents the ruling. This categorical distinction is the core of the Hanafi argument.
The ruling anatomical difference is rooted in the nature of the act, not merely a semantic preference.

الفارق ہے۔
It does not indicate; so the absence of a description would not indicate the absence of a ruling a fortiori. Now as for why the absence of a condition does not indicate the absence of a ruling, the answer is that the condition 'if you enter the house' enters upon the cause, i.e., 'you are divorced', not upon the ruling, because the ruling is the occurrence of divorce, which is not mentioned here. Therefore, when the condition entered upon the cause, the absence of the condition resulted in the absence of the cause, i.e., the condition was not found, so the cause was not contracted, and when the cause was not contracted, the ruling was not found. So the absence of the ruling resulted from the absence of the cause, not from the absence of the condition. Thus it is understood that the absence of the condition does not cause the absence of the ruling. Hence the claim that suspension by condition is a preventative of the ruling is proven false, and the absence of the condition resulted in the absence of the cause, i.e., the cause did not contract due to the condition. So it is established that suspension by condition is a preventative of the cause, not a preventative of the ruling, as is the Hanafi doctrine. For clarification: in 'if you enter the house, you are divorced', entering the house is the condition and 'you are divorced' is the cause. The cause 'you are divorced' is suspended upon the condition 'if you enter the house'. If he did not suspend the cause upon the condition and simply said 'you are divorced', the cause would contract immediately and divorce would occur. But when he suspended it with the condition, the condition prevented the cause from connecting to its locus, which is the woman. And when the cause did not connect to its locus and did not occur upon the locus, it did not become a cause at all. So it is as if, before the condition is found, 'you are divorced' did not become a cause. And when 'you are divorced' did not become a cause, the ruling did not occur. So the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the cause, not due to the absence of the condition. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentions a case supporting the Hanafi saying that suspension prevents the cause, not the ruling, and refutes Imam al-Shafi'i. This case is also agreed upon. He says: O master! According to you, suspension prevents the ruling, not the cause, meaning the cause exists but the ruling is not found due to the condition. So what do you say in this case? If someone swore an oath: 'By Allah, I will not divorce my wife,' and then gave a suspended divorce, saying: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' Here, 'you are divorced' is the cause, 'if you enter the house' is the condition, and the occurrence of divorce is the ruling. According to your statement, suspension prevents the ruling, not the cause. Therefore, the cause, i.e., 'you are divorced', should contract, and divorce should occur to the woman. Yet you also say that divorce will not occur. And when divorce does not occur, it clearly means that one must admit that suspension by condition prevents the cause, not the ruling, as is the Hanafi doctrine. And this is unlike the option of condition in sale, because the option of condition enters upon the ruling, not upon the cause. For this reason, if someone swore an oath that he would not sell, and then sold with an option of condition, he would be sinful (i.e., break his oath). Translation: And this is unlike the option of condition in sale, because the option of condition enters upon the ruling, not upon the cause. For this reason, if someone swore an oath that he would not sell, and then sold with an option of condition, he would be sinful. Explanation: The author is responding to an argument of Imam al-Shafi'i. Imam al-Shafi'i says that suspension by condition prevents the ruling, not the cause. For example, if someone sold with an option of condition, the sale is contracted but execution of ownership does not occur. So the option of condition enters upon the ruling of sale (ownership), not upon the cause (the contract). Thus it is understood that the condition prevents the ruling, not the cause. Imam al-Shafi'i analogized divorce and manumission to sale. The author responds: You have spoken correctly that the option of condition prevents the ruling (ownership), not the cause, i.e., the contract. But your analogy of one to the other is not correct; it is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma' al-fariq). Because sale is of the category of affirmation (ithbat); in it, the sold item is affirmed for the buyer and ownership of the price is affirmed for the seller. And a thing that is of the category of affirmation does not permit suspension because suspension implies gambling (qimar), since it is suspended on a condition: it is unknown whether the condition will occur or not; if it occurs, the sale occurs, otherwise not, and this is gambling. Gambling is forbidden. Therefore, the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a sale with a condition. Thus, by analogy, the option of condition would not be permissible in sale. However, the option of condition, contrary to analogy, is established by hadith to protect people from fraud and deception. So it is established by necessity, and thus it remains only to the extent of necessity. And here, considering the condition as entering upon the ruling fulfills the need, while considering it as entering upon the cause is an additional requirement, because considering it as entering upon the cause would negate two things: the cause itself and consequently the ruling, whereas the need is fulfilled by merely negating the ruling. Therefore, it is considered as entering upon the ruling so that the effect of the condition is minimal and the need is met. And since the option of condition enters upon the ruling, not upon the sale, if someone swore that he would not sell and then sold with an option of condition, he would be sinful because the sale is contracted with the option. As for the case of divorce and manumission, they are of the category of removal (isqat); in them, ownership of marriage and ownership of the slave are revoked. Therefore, in these, suspension does not imply gambling. So here, suspension prevents the cause, not the ruling. Thus, the two are different; to analogize one to the other is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma' al-fariq).
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul al-fiqh dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding the nature of conditional suspension (ta'liq bil-shart). The author refutes the Shafi'i claim that a condition prevents the ruling (hukm) rather than the cause (sabab). The Hanafi position is that in cases of divorce and manumission, the condition prevents the cause itself from coming into existence, so the ruling never arises. The author begins by explaining why absence of condition does not indicate absence of ruling: the condition is attached to the cause, not the ruling. When the condition is absent, the cause does not form; hence the ruling is absent due to the absence of cause. Then the author presents a concrete case agreed upon by both schools: a man swears not to divorce his wife, then says 'if you enter the house, you are divorced.' According to the Shafi'i view (that condition prevents ruling only), the cause 'you are divorced' would be effective and divorce should occur, violating his oath. But even Shafi'is agree divorce does not occur here. This proves that the condition prevented the cause from forming, not just the ruling. The author then distinguishes this from khiyar al-shart (option of condition) in sales. In sales, the option condition enters upon the ruling (ownership) not the cause (the contract). This is because sale is an act of affirmation (ithbat); suspending it on a condition would imply gambling, which is prohibited. Yet the option of condition is allowed by hadith to prevent fraud, so it is tolerated by necessity. In divorce and manumission, which are acts of removal (isqat), suspension does not involve gambling, so the original rule applies: the condition prevents the cause. Thus the two cases are fundamentally different, and analogizing them is a false analogy (qiyas ma' al-fariq).

Key Terms
تعلیق بالشرط (ta'liq bil-shart): Suspending the occurrence of a legal act (like divorce) upon the fulfillment of a condition.
مانع سبب (mani' sabab): Something that prevents the cause from coming into existence or being effective.
مانع حکم (mani' hukm): Something that prevents the ruling from applying, though the cause exists.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one party to cancel within a specified period.
قیاس مع الفارق (qiyas ma' al-fariq): An analogy that fails because the two cases are fundamentally different in their legal nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, does a conditional suspension (ta'liq) in divorce prevent the cause (sabab) or the ruling (hukm)?
A) It prevents the cause (sabab).
B) It prevents the ruling (hukm).
C) It prevents both the cause and the ruling.
D) It prevents neither.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, conditional suspension in acts of removal (isqat) like divorce prevents the cause, while in acts of affirmation (ithbat) like sale it prevents the ruling — a distinction rooted in the prohibition of gambling.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Ta'liq and the Categorical Distinction: Hanafi Refutation of Shafi'i's Qiyas
Hanafi response to Shafi'i's qiyas on ta'liq and khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts of ithbat and isqat, apply the Hanafi principle of ta'liq as man'i sabab in isqat, and evaluate the Shafi'i analogy as qiyas ma'a al-fariq.

Core Idea
Ta'liq is man'i sabab in isqat, man'i hukm in ithbat by istihsan — the category determines the ruling.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi usul argument against Imam al-Shafi'i's analogical reasoning. The core issue is whether conditional suspension (ta'liq) prevents the cause (sabab) of a legal act or merely prevents its effect (hukm). By distinguishing between acts that establish rights (ithbat) and those that annul rights (isqat), the Hanafi school shows that a single analogy cannot cover both domains. You will learn how this distinction resolves apparent contradictions between khiyar al-shart in sale and suspended talaq, and why expiation before breaking an oath is invalid in the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i Position on Ta'liq
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that ta'liq (suspension by condition) is a preventer of the ruling (man'i hukm), not the cause. His evidence: in khiyar al-shart, the sale is concluded (cause exists) but ownership is not executed (ruling is prevented). He then analogizes talaq and itq to sale, claiming that ta'liq should likewise prevent the ruling in those acts.
Note: Shafi'i's qiyas treats sale, talaq, and itq as similar in that all are dispositions with a cause and ruling.
2
State the Hanafi Response: Categorical Distinction
The Hanafi author rejects Shafi'i's analogy as qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference). Sale is an act of ithbat (establishment) — it creates ownership rights. Talaq and itq are isqat (annulment) — they remove rights. This categorical difference makes the analogy invalid.
Key: Ta'liq in ithbat leads to qimar (gambling) because the outcome is uncertain; in isqat, the effect is simply delayed, with no gambling implication.
3
Analyze the Exception: Khiyar al-Shart in Sale
Although ta'liq in sale would normally be qimar, khiyar al-shart is an exception established by hadith (istihsan) to protect people from harm. It is allowed only to the extent of necessity, and it prevents only the ruling, not the cause. This preserves the principle that ta'liq in ithbat is otherwise invalid.
Note: The prohibition 'Nahana an bay'i wa shart' shows the default, but the hadith on khiyar creates a limited exception.
4
Derive the Implications for Talaq and Itq
Since talaq and itq are isqat, ta'liq here is a preventer of the cause (man'i sabab). The utterance 'If I marry you, you are divorced' does not take effect immediately; the cause (the divorce pronouncement) is suspended until the condition occurs. When the condition occurs, the effect arises as if uttered at that moment. Thus suspended talaq is valid.
This also means that paying expiation (kaffara) before breaking the oath is invalid because the cause for expiation (breaking the oath) has not yet occurred.
5
Evaluate the Refutation of Shafi'i's Mali/Badani Distinction
Shafi'i attempted to distinguish between financial (mali) and physical (badani) acts, but the Hanafi author rejects this: in financial matters, Allah's right is the act of performance, and wealth is a tool; in rights of servants, the specific wealth is intended. This does not affect the ta'liq principle.
The distinction is 'sāqiṭ' (invalid) because it does not change the nature of ithbat vs isqat.
